❓:6. Given the complex number z satisfies(a-2) z^{2018}+a z^{2017} mathrm{i}+a z mathrm{i}+2-a=0 text {, }where, a<1, mathrm{i}=sqrt{-1}. Then |z|= qquad

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I need to find the modulus of the complex number z given this equation: (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a z²⁰¹⁷ i + a z i + 2 - a = 0, where a is less than 1. Hmm, complex numbers can sometimes be tricky, especially with such high exponents. Let me try to break this down step by step.First, let me rewrite the equation to make sure I have it correctly:(a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) = 0.So, the equation is a polynomial in z with complex coefficients. The exponents are 2018, 2017, 1, and 0 (since the last term is a constant). Since the exponents are so large, directly solving for z might not be feasible. Maybe there's a pattern or a substitution that can simplify things?Let me see... The exponents go from 2018 down to 1, but there's a gap between z²⁰¹⁷ and z. Wait, maybe factoring? If I can factor out some common terms, that might help. Let's look at the terms:First term: (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸Second term: a i z²⁰¹⁷Third term: a i zFourth term: (2 - a)Hmm. The first and last terms have coefficients (a - 2) and (2 - a), which are negatives of each other. That's interesting. Maybe there's a way to pair these terms?Also, the second and third terms both have a i as coefficients. The exponents on z are 2017 and 1. Maybe there's some symmetry here? If I factor out z from the second and third terms, what happens?Wait, let's try to group the terms:Group 1: (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + (2 - a)Group 2: a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i zSo, group 1 is (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + (2 - a) = (a - 2)(z²⁰¹⁸ - 1), because (2 - a) is -(a - 2). So, factoring out (a - 2) gives:(a - 2)(z²⁰¹⁸ - 1)Similarly, group 2 is a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z = a i z (z²⁰¹⁶ + 1). Wait, let's check that:a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z = a i z (z²⁰¹⁶ + 1). Yes, because z²⁰¹⁷ = z * z²⁰¹⁶. So, factoring out a i z gives a i z (z²⁰¹⁶ + 1).So, putting the two groups together, the equation becomes:(a - 2)(z²⁰¹⁸ - 1) + a i z (z²⁰¹⁶ + 1) = 0.Hmm, that seems better. Let me write that again:(a - 2)(z²⁰¹⁸ - 1) + a i z (z²⁰¹⁶ + 1) = 0.Now, notice that z²⁰¹⁸ is (z²⁰¹⁶) * z². Maybe substituting w = z²⁰¹⁶ or something? Let me see. Let's denote w = z²⁰¹⁶. Then z²⁰¹⁸ = z² * w, and z²⁰¹⁶ = w. But I'm not sure if this substitution helps.Alternatively, perhaps factor z²⁰¹⁶ from the second term. Wait, the second term is a i z (z²⁰¹⁶ + 1). Hmm. So, maybe express z²⁰¹⁸ - 1 as z²(z²⁰¹⁶) - 1. Let's try that:z²⁰¹⁸ - 1 = z² * z²⁰¹⁶ - 1 = z² * w - 1, where w = z²⁰¹⁶.But perhaps not helpful yet.Alternatively, maybe factor the equation differently. Let me see:Original equation after grouping:(a - 2)(z²⁰¹⁸ - 1) + a i z (z²⁰¹⁶ + 1) = 0.Let me factor z²⁰¹⁶ from the second term's parentheses:z²⁰¹⁶ + 1 = z²⁰¹⁶ + 1. Hmm, unless there's a common factor with z²⁰¹⁸ - 1. Let's note that z²⁰¹⁸ - 1 is (z²)^1009 - 1, which factors as (z² - 1)(z²⁰¹⁶ + z²⁰¹⁴ + ... + z² + 1). But that seems complicated. Maybe not useful here.Alternatively, note that z²⁰¹⁸ = (z²⁰¹⁶)(z²). So, perhaps write the first group as (a - 2)(z² * z²⁰¹⁶ - 1). So:(a - 2)(z² * z²⁰¹⁶ - 1) + a i z (z²⁰¹⁶ + 1) = 0.If I let w = z²⁰¹⁶, then:(a - 2)(z² w - 1) + a i z (w + 1) = 0.But now, this introduces two variables, z and w, unless there's a relation between w and z. But since w = z²⁰¹⁶, unless we can express z² in terms of w, but z² would be w^(1/1008), which might not be helpful.Alternatively, perhaps take modulus on both sides? Wait, but the equation is a complex equation, so taking modulus might lose information. Hmm. But maybe if I can write the equation as A + B = 0, then |A| = |B|, but that might not directly help.Alternatively, suppose that |z| = r, which is what we need to find. Maybe if we can write the equation in terms of r, then solve for r. Let me try that approach.Let |z| = r. Then |z²⁰¹⁸| = r²⁰¹⁸, |z²⁰¹⁷| = r²⁰¹⁷, |z| = r. But since the equation is complex, perhaps taking modulus of both sides would require knowing the arguments. Alternatively, if z is a root, then maybe the equation relates z and its conjugate? Not sure.Alternatively, if z ≠ 0, maybe we can divide the equation by z²⁰¹⁷? Let's see. Let's try dividing each term by z²⁰¹⁷:(a - 2) z + a i + a i z^(-2016) + (2 - a) z^(-2017) = 0.Hmm, but z^(-2016) is 1/z²⁰¹⁶, which complicates things. Unless |z| = 1, in which case z^(-k) is the conjugate of z^k. Wait, if |z| = 1, then |z|^k = 1, so z^k has modulus 1, and z^(-k) = 1/z^k = overline{z^k} since |z| = 1. But if |z| = 1, then maybe that's a possible case. Maybe the modulus is 1? Let me check if that's possible.Assume |z| = 1. Then z overline{z} = 1, so overline{z} = 1/z.But how does that help here? Let me see. If |z| = 1, then perhaps we can take the conjugate of the entire equation and relate it to the original equation. Let me try that.Given the original equation:(a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) = 0.Take complex conjugate of both sides:(a - 2) overline{z²⁰¹⁸} + a (-i) overline{z²⁰¹⁷} + a (-i) overline{z} + (2 - a) = 0.Because the conjugate of i is -i, and since a is real (I assume a is real because it's not specified as complex), the conjugate of (a - 2) is itself. Also, the conjugate of z²⁰¹⁸ is overline{z}²⁰¹⁸, and since |z| = 1, overline{z} = 1/z, so overline{z²⁰¹⁸} = (1/z)²⁰¹⁸ = z^{-2018}.Similarly, overline{z²⁰¹⁷} = z^{-2017}, and overline{z} = z^{-1}.So substituting these into the conjugate equation:(a - 2) z^{-2018} - a i z^{-2017} - a i z^{-1} + (2 - a) = 0.Now, multiply both sides by z²⁰¹⁸ to eliminate denominators:(a - 2) - a i z - a i z²⁰¹⁷ + (2 - a) z²⁰¹⁸ = 0.But this seems similar to the original equation. Let me compare the original equation:Original: (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) = 0.Conjugate processed: (a - 2) - a i z - a i z²⁰¹⁷ + (2 - a) z²⁰¹⁸ = 0.If we rearrange terms:(2 - a) z²⁰¹⁸ + (-a i) z²⁰¹⁷ + (-a i) z + (a - 2) = 0.Comparing this to the original equation:Original: (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z + (2 - a) = 0.Wait, actually, no. The coefficients are different. The original equation has (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) = 0, whereas the conjugated and multiplied equation has (2 - a) z²⁰¹⁸ - a i z²⁰¹⁷ - a i z + (a - 2) = 0.If we multiply the original equation by -1:- (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ - a i z²⁰¹⁷ - a i z - (2 - a) = 0,Which is:(2 - a) z²⁰¹⁸ - a i z²⁰¹⁷ - a i z + (a - 2) = 0.Which is exactly the conjugated equation. Therefore, if the original equation holds, then the conjugated equation is equivalent to the original equation multiplied by -1. Therefore, the two equations are compatible. So, if z is a solution with |z| = 1, then its conjugate equation is just -1 times the original equation. So, this suggests that if |z| = 1, then the equation is consistent in some way.But does this necessarily mean that |z| must be 1? Maybe. Let's test if |z| = 1 is a solution.Assume |z| = 1. Then, let's see if the equation can be satisfied. Since |z| = 1, z^{-1} = overline{z}. Let me try to express the equation in terms of z and its conjugate.But maybe there's a better approach. Let's suppose |z| = 1, then we can write z = e^{iθ}, but that might complicate things with such high exponents. Alternatively, let's check if z = 1 is a solution. If z = 1, then plug into the equation:(a - 2)(1) + a i (1) + a i (1) + (2 - a) = (a - 2 + 2 - a) + a i + a i = 0 + 2 a i. Which is 2 a i. But this must be zero, so 2 a i = 0 implies a = 0. But a < 1, so a could be zero. So if a = 0, then z = 1 is a solution. But the problem states "given the complex number z satisfies the equation for a < 1", so maybe z could be 1 when a = 0, but we need a general solution for any a < 1.Similarly, try z = -1:(a - 2)(-1)^2018 + a i (-1)^2017 + a i (-1) + 2 - a.Since (-1)^2018 = 1, (-1)^2017 = -1. So:(a - 2)(1) + a i (-1) + a i (-1) + 2 - a = (a - 2 - 2a) + (-a i - a i) = (-a -2) + (-2 a i). This equals - (a + 2) - 2 a i. For this to be zero, both real and imaginary parts must be zero. So:- (a + 2) = 0 ⇒ a = -2,and -2 a i = 0 ⇒ a = 0. Contradiction. So z = -1 is not a solution unless a is both -2 and 0, which is impossible. Therefore, z = -1 is not a solution.Alternatively, maybe |z| = 1 is the answer. Let me try to verify that.Suppose |z| = 1. Then, as mentioned earlier, z^{-1} = overline{z}. Let me see if I can manipulate the original equation using this.Original equation:(a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) = 0.Let me factor out z²⁰¹⁷ from the first two terms:z²⁰¹⁷ [ (a - 2) z + a i ] + a i z + (2 - a) = 0.But if |z| = 1, then z²⁰¹⁷ = z^{-1}, since z²⁰¹⁸ = z^{2018} = (z^{2017}) z = z^{-1} * z = 1? Wait, no. Wait, if |z| = 1, then z^{2018} = z^{2018 mod 4} because i cycles every 4. Wait, but 2018 divided by 4 is 504*4 + 2, so z^2018 = z². Wait, that's only if z is a root of unity, but we don't know that. Hmm, maybe that's not helpful.Alternatively, multiply both sides by z. Let's try that.Multiplying the original equation by z:(a - 2) z²⁰¹⁹ + a i z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z² + (2 - a) z = 0.But not sure how this helps. Let me see the original equation and the multiplied one:Original: (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) = 0.After multiplying by z: (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁹ + a i z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z² + (2 - a) z = 0.Compare the two equations. Maybe subtract or combine them?Let me denote the original equation as Eq1 and the multiplied one as Eq2.If I subtract Eq1 multiplied by z from Eq2, but that might not help. Alternatively, perhaps express z²⁰¹⁹ in terms of lower exponents.Alternatively, notice that 2019 = 2017 + 2, but not sure. This seems too vague.Wait, maybe if |z| = 1, then z^{2018} = overline{z}^2, since z^{2018} = z^{-2} (because |z| = 1 implies z^{2018} * z^2 = z^{2020} = (z^4)^{505} = 1^{505} = 1, so z^{2018} = z^{-2}). Wait, 2018 + 2 = 2020, which is divisible by 4? Wait, 2020 divided by 4 is 505, yes. So if z^4 = 1, then z^{2020} = 1. But we don't know if z is a 4th root of unity. So this approach might not work.Alternatively, perhaps think in terms of reciprocal polynomials. If |z| = 1, then the polynomial should satisfy some reciprocal property. Let me check the polynomial.The given equation is:(a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) = 0.If we reverse the coefficients, the polynomial would be:(2 - a) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + (a - 2).But not sure if this is a reciprocal polynomial. A reciprocal polynomial satisfies P(z) = z^n P(1/z), where n is the degree. Let's check:The original polynomial is of degree 2018. Let's compute z²⁰¹⁸ P(1/z):z²⁰¹⁸ P(1/z) = z²⁰¹⁸ [ (a - 2)(1/z)^2018 + a i (1/z)^2017 + a i (1/z) + (2 - a) ]= z²⁰¹⁸ [ (a - 2) z^{-2018} + a i z^{-2017} + a i z^{-1} + (2 - a) ]= (a - 2) + a i z + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + (2 - a) z²⁰¹⁸.Comparing this with the original polynomial P(z) = (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a), we see that z²⁰¹⁸ P(1/z) = (2 - a) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (a - 2).Which is almost the same as P(z) except the constant term and the leading coefficient are swapped and sign-changed. Specifically, the original leading term is (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ and the constant term is (2 - a), while in the reciprocal polynomial, the leading term is (2 - a) z²⁰¹⁸ and the constant term is (a - 2). Therefore, unless (a - 2) = (2 - a), which would require a - 2 = 2 - a ⇒ 2a = 4 ⇒ a = 2, but a < 1, so this is not the case. Therefore, the polynomial is not reciprocal unless a = 2, which is excluded. Therefore, reciprocal polynomial approach may not help here.Alternatively, let's assume |z| = 1 and see if the equation can hold. If |z| = 1, then we can write z = e^{iθ}. Plugging into the equation:(a - 2) e^{i 2018 θ} + a i e^{i 2017 θ} + a i e^{i θ} + (2 - a) = 0.This is a complex equation. For this to hold, both the real and imaginary parts must be zero. Let's separate them.Let me denote e^{i 2018 θ} = cos(2018θ) + i sin(2018θ),e^{i 2017 θ} = cos(2017θ) + i sin(2017θ),e^{i θ} = cosθ + i sinθ.Substituting into the equation:(a - 2)(cos2018θ + i sin2018θ) + a i (cos2017θ + i sin2017θ) + a i (cosθ + i sinθ) + (2 - a) = 0.Expanding each term:First term: (a - 2)cos2018θ + i (a - 2) sin2018θ.Second term: a i cos2017θ + a i^2 sin2017θ = a i cos2017θ - a sin2017θ.Third term: a i cosθ + a i^2 sinθ = a i cosθ - a sinθ.Fourth term: (2 - a).Combine all real and imaginary parts:Real parts:(a - 2)cos2018θ - a sin2017θ - a sinθ + (2 - a).Imaginary parts:(a - 2) sin2018θ + a cos2017θ + a cosθ.For the equation to hold, both real and imaginary parts must be zero. So:Real part:(a - 2)cos2018θ - a sin2017θ - a sinθ + (2 - a) = 0.Imaginary part:(a - 2) sin2018θ + a cos2017θ + a cosθ = 0.These equations look complicated. Maybe there's a specific θ that satisfies both? For example, θ = 0, θ = π, etc. Let's try θ = 0:θ = 0:Real part:(a - 2)cos0 - a sin0 - a sin0 + (2 - a) = (a - 2)(1) - 0 - 0 + (2 - a) = (a - 2 + 2 - a) = 0.Imaginary part:(a - 2) sin0 + a cos0 + a cos0 = 0 + a(1) + a(1) = 2a. So 2a = 0 ⇒ a = 0. So when a = 0, θ = 0 (i.e., z = 1) is a solution. Which matches our earlier check. But a can be any value less than 1, so this is only valid for a = 0.Similarly, try θ = π:z = e^{iπ} = -1. Let's compute:Real part:(a - 2)cos(2018π) - a sin(2017π) - a sinπ + (2 - a).cos(2018π) = cos(2*1009π) = 1.sin(2017π) = sin(π*(2017)) = sin(π*(2*1008 + 1)) = sin(π) = 0.Similarly, sinπ = 0.So Real part: (a - 2)(1) - 0 - 0 + (2 - a) = (a - 2 + 2 - a) = 0.Imaginary part:(a - 2) sin(2018π) + a cos(2017π) + a cosπ.sin(2018π) = 0.cos(2017π) = cos(π*(2*1008 + 1)) = cos(π) = -1.cosπ = -1.So Imaginary part: 0 + a*(-1) + a*(-1) = -a -a = -2a. For this to be zero, -2a = 0 ⇒ a = 0. So again, only a = 0.So θ = 0 and θ = π give solutions only when a = 0. Since a < 1, we need a solution for general a < 1. Maybe there's another θ that works? But solving those equations for θ seems very difficult. So perhaps |z| = 1 is not necessarily the case, unless forced by the equation.Alternatively, maybe |z| must be 1. Let me consider taking modulus on both sides of the original equation.Original equation:(a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) = 0.Take modulus:| (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) | = 0.Which implies that the modulus is zero, so the expression inside must be zero, which we already know. But modulus zero means the expression itself is zero. But how does that help? Perhaps not directly.But maybe use the triangle inequality. Let me try to write:| (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ | + | a i z²⁰¹⁷ | + | a i z | + | (2 - a) | ≥ | (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + a i z²⁰¹⁷ + a i z + (2 - a) | = 0.But the sum of moduli is greater or equal to zero, which is always true. Not helpful.Alternatively, if we suppose that |z| ≠ 1, then the terms with high exponents would dominate if |z| > 1 or tend to zero if |z| < 1. Since the equation equals zero, maybe balance between the terms.Let me assume |z| ≠ 1 and see what happens.Case 1: |z| > 1.Then |z|^{2018} is much larger than |z|^{2017}, |z|, and 1. So the dominant term would be (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸. If |z| > 1, then | (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ | would be large because |z|^{2018} is large, and the other terms would be smaller in comparison. But their sum is zero, so this would require cancellation. However, since a < 1, (a - 2) is negative (since a - 2 < 1 - 2 = -1). So (a - 2) is a negative real coefficient. The term (a - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ has modulus |a - 2| |z|^{2018}. The other terms have moduli |a| |z|^{2017}, |a| |z|, and |2 - a|. If |z| is very large, the first term would dominate, and since it's non-zero, the sum couldn't be zero. Therefore, |z| cannot be greater than 1.Case 2: |z| < 1.Then |z|^{2018} is very small, as well as |z|^{2017}, |z|. The dominant term would be the constant term (2 - a). Since a < 1, 2 - a is positive (greater than 1). So the constant term is positive, and the other terms are smaller. Therefore, their sum would be approximately (2 - a) ≠ 0. Therefore, it's unlikely that |z| < 1 would satisfy the equation.Hence, the only possible case is |z| = 1. Because if |z| > 1, the highest degree term dominates and can't be canceled; if |z| < 1, the constant term dominates and can't be canceled. Therefore, |z| must be 1. Therefore, the modulus |z| is 1.But wait, let me verify this reasoning. If |z| = 1, then all terms have modulus |a - 2|, |a|, |a|, |2 - a|. But since a < 1, (a - 2) is negative, |a - 2| = 2 - a. So the coefficients' moduli are (2 - a), |a|, |a|, (2 - a). So the equation is a combination of terms with modulus 2 - a and a. So it's possible for them to cancel each other out. For example, if z is chosen such that the terms interfere destructively.But according to the previous cases, if |z| ≠ 1, then the equation cannot hold because one term would dominate. Therefore, the only possibility is |z| = 1. Therefore, the answer is |z| = 1.But just to be thorough, let's test with a specific example where a = 0, which is allowed since a < 1. If a = 0, the equation becomes:(0 - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + 0 + 0 + 2 - 0 = 0 ⇒ -2 z²⁰¹⁸ + 2 = 0 ⇒ z²⁰¹⁸ = 1 ⇒ |z| = 1, since |z²⁰¹⁸| = |z|^{2018} = 1 ⇒ |z| = 1. So when a = 0, |z| = 1. That checks out.Another example, take a = 1/2 (which is less than 1). Then the equation is:(1/2 - 2) z²⁰¹⁸ + (1/2) i z²⁰¹⁷ + (1/2) i z + (2 - 1/2) = 0 ⇒ (-3/2) z²⁰¹⁸ + (1/2) i z²⁰¹⁷ + (1/2) i z + 3/2 = 0.If |z| = 1, let's see if there exists such a z. For example, take z = 1:Left-hand side: (-3/2)(1) + (1/2)i(1) + (1/2)i(1) + 3/2 = (-3/2 + 3/2) + i(1/2 + 1/2) = 0 + i = i ≠ 0. 🔑:Given that α∈({frac{π}{2},π}) and cos({π-α})=frac{3}{5}, let's find tan alpha step by step.1. Using the Cosine of the Supplement: The cosine of the supplement identity states that cos(π-α) = -cos(α). Given cos({π-α})=frac{3}{5}, we can write: [ -cos(α) = frac{3}{5} implies cos(α) = -frac{3}{5} ]2. Finding sin(α): Since α is in the interval (frac{π}{2}, π), sin(α) is positive. Using the Pythagorean identity sin^2(α) + cos^2(α) = 1, we find sin(α): [ sin^2(α) = 1 - cos^2(α) = 1 - left(-frac{3}{5}right)^2 = 1 - frac{9}{25} = frac{16}{25} ] Taking the square root and considering the positive value for sin(α), we get: [ sin(α) = sqrt{frac{16}{25}} = frac{4}{5} ]3. Calculating tan(α): Finally, we use the definition of tangent, which is the ratio of sine to cosine: [ tan(α) = frac{sin(α)}{cos(α)} = frac{frac{4}{5}}{-frac{3}{5}} = -frac{4}{3} ]Therefore, the value of tan alpha is boxed{-frac{4}{3}}.

❓:4. Let the function y=f(x) have a domain of mathbf{R}, and for x>1, and for any real numbers x, y in mathbf{R}, f(x+y)=f(x) f(y) holds. (2003 Hunan Province Mathematics Competition Problem)

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I have this problem here from the 2003 Hunan Province Mathematics Competition. Let me try to parse it step by step. The problem has a few parts: a function f(x) with certain properties, a sequence defined in terms of f, and an inequality involving the product of terms with the sequence. The goal is to find the minimum value of k such that the inequality holds for all natural numbers n.First, let's break down the given information:1. The function y = f(x) has a domain of all real numbers R, and for x > 1, it's defined. 🔑:Solve the confusion ofbegin{array}{l}1990=: 2^{10}+2^{9}+2^{8}+2^{7}+2^{6}+0 cdot 2^{5} +0 cdot 2^{4}+0 cdot 2^{3}+2^{2}+2^{1}+0 cdot 2^{0},end{array}and write 1,2, cdots, 1989 in binary form. The operation is as follows:First, take away 2^{10}=1024 stones from each pile that has enough; second, take away 2^{9}= 512 stones from each pile that has enough; …… finally, take away the stones from the piles that have only 2^{0}=1 stone left. This way, a total of 11 operations are used.Since there are piles with only one stone, there must be an operation to take one stone; if the remaining operations all take more than 2 stones, then the piles with exactly two stones cannot be taken away. Therefore, 2 operations can take at most 1+2=3 stones, ..., 10 operations can take at most 1+2+ 2^{2}+cdots+2^{9}=1023 stones. Thus, 1950 piles of stones require at least 11 operations.

We use the shoelace formula to calculate the area of this parallelogram.The coordinates of the midpoints are ((-3,0)), ((2,0)), ((5,4)), and ((0,4)). Applying the shoelace formula:[text{Area} = frac{1}{2} left| (-3 cdot 0 + 2 cdot 4 + 5 cdot 4 + 0 cdot 0) - (0 cdot 2 + 0 cdot 5 + 4 cdot 0 + 4 cdot -3) right|]Calculating the terms:[text{Sum of products} = (-3 cdot 0) + (2 cdot 4) + (5 cdot 4) + (0 cdot 0) = 0 + 8 + 20 + 0 = 28][text{Sum of cross products} = (0 cdot 2) + (0 cdot 5) + (4 cdot 0) + (4 cdot -3) = 0 + 0 + 0 - 12 = -12][text{Area} = frac{1}{2} left| 28 - (-12) right| = frac{1}{2} left| 40 right| = 20]The area of the Varignon parallelogram is 20. Since this area is half the area of the original rectangle, we double it:[text{Area of the rectangle} = 20 times 2 = 40]Thus, the area of the rectangle is (boxed{C}).]

🔑:The midpoints of the four sides of every rectangle are the vertices of a rhombus whose area is half the area of the rectangle: Note that the diagonals of the rhombus have the same lengths as the sides of the rectangle.Let A=(-3,0), B=(2,0), C=(5,4), and D=(0,4). Note that A,B,C, and D are the vertices of a rhombus whose diagonals have lengths AC=4sqrt{5} and BD=2sqrt{5}. It follows that the dimensions of the rectangle are 4sqrt{5} and 2sqrt{5}, so the area of the rectangle is 4sqrt{5}cdot2sqrt{5}=boxed{textbf{(C) } 40}.~MRENTHUSIASM

❓:3. Find the remainder of the division of the polynomial P(x) by the polynomial x^{2}+7 x+12, given that P(-4)=2 and P(-3)=1.Examples of how to write the answer:2 mathrm{x}-3mathrm{x}+4

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I need to find the remainder when the polynomial P(x) is divided by x² + 7x + 12. The problem also gives me two values: P(-4) = 2 and P(-3) = 1. Hmm, let me think about how to approach this.First, I remember that when you divide a polynomial by a quadratic, the remainder will be a first-degree polynomial, right? So the remainder should look like ax + b, where a and b are constants that we need to find. Another thing I recall is the Remainder Theorem, but that usually applies when dividing by linear divisors like (x - c). But maybe there's a generalized version for higher degrees. Wait, yes! The Polynomial Remainder Theorem states that the remainder of dividing a polynomial P(x) by another polynomial D(x) is the same as P(x) evaluated at the roots of D(x). But since D(x) here is quadratic, it has two roots, which are the solutions to x² + 7x + 12 = 0. Let me factor that quadratic to find the roots.Factoring x² + 7x + 12: we need two numbers that multiply to 12 and add to 7. That would be 3 and 4. So the quadratic factors as (x + 3)(x + 4). Therefore, the roots are x = -3 and x = -4. So, according to the Remainder Theorem, if I divide P(x) by (x + 3)(x + 4), the remainder R(x) should satisfy R(-3) = P(-3) and R(-4) = P(-4). Since R(x) is a linear polynomial ax + b, we can set up two equations using the given values:1. When x = -4: R(-4) = a*(-4) + b = -4a + b = P(-4) = 22. When x = -3: R(-3) = a*(-3) + b = -3a + b = P(-3) = 1Now I have a system of two equations:-4a + b = 2 -3a + b = 1I can solve this system for a and b. Let's subtract the second equation from the first to eliminate b:(-4a + b) - (-3a + b) = 2 - 1 (-4a + b + 3a - b) = 1 (-a) = 1 So, a = -1Now plug a = -1 into one of the equations to find b. Let's use the second equation:-3*(-1) + b = 1 3 + b = 1 b = 1 - 3 b = -2Therefore, the remainder R(x) is -1x - 2, which simplifies to -x - 2.
